Subject: Tire recommendations for a Porsche 944 Turbo S

I just purchased a 1989 Porsche 944 Turbo S and I am seeking advice on what
tires I should buy to replace the original rear tires, 245/45-ZR16 Goodyear 
Eagles, which have worn out after 19,000 miles.  As I recall, Goodyear
Eagles (except for the soft ``S'' compound tires) have not fared well
in recent tire tests in Car & Driver and Road & Track.  But I have 
not seen any tests of the ZR rated Eagles rather the VR tires.  I want
to replace them with tires that perform well on both dry and wet pavement
(with more emphasis on dry performance), generate less road noise (if
possible) than the Eagles, and wear as well as the Eagles.  The suspension 
in the 944 Turbo S is stiff and transmits a lot of tire noise, which
I would like to minimize as much as I can without compromising performance
(relative to the original equipment tires).

I would appreciate any information or advice that subscribers to this list
can provide.  My email address is cork@rice.edu .

Subject: Tires for 951S

I highly recommend COMP TAs (Street) for the 951.  I have used them,
Dunlop D40M2, Pirellis, and bridgestones RE71 (both street and track).
The BStones are best in the wet, but do not wear nearly as well as the
Comp TAs, and dry traction is comparable.

The one main advantage to comps is the price.  Tire Rack has the set for
$516 + shipping (about $5 per tire, arrival in 2 days IN to DE)

DunSlop is $646.  I will never own them again.  They wear almost as
quicky as race tires (6,000 miles, 4 track days) but they do perform
well.  I just hate the company after having a tire de-tread and ruining
my fender.

BStone $716.  Nice tire.  Wear fair.  Great in wet.  Used to but them
shaved for $100 each, but lietzinger only shaves the R compound now.

Anyway, those prices are pretty representative.  An interesting note:
CompTAs are just as expensive as RE71a if not moreso everywhere but tire
rack.  All prices are for 245-45ZR16 and 225-50ZR16s.

Steve

Subject: DE Newsletter Article

A newsletter article from the Delaware PCA region follows.  It is about
2 pages and written by some bozo.
------------------------------------------------------------------
Good Riddance to 1990!

I don't know about the rest of you, but 1990 was a year I'm glad to get behind
me.  Car troubles were unprecedented in a 12 month period, and I was only
really responsible for a few of them!

In January, I took my father to Summit Point for a weekend of fun.  I managed
to get him signed up as a "student".  Actually I bought him passenger rights as
I had overpaid for the Car Guys Refrigerator Bowl series.  The weekend went
fantastically, the car was quick (cold temperatures make for Happy Boost as the
intercooler efficiency increases) and I racked up gobs of miles.  I took Dad out
for a run and he was rather amazed.  So much so that he looked a bit green and
had to sit on a guard rail for about 20 minutes before regaining control of his
speaking faculties.  Finally he mumbled something about fast and turning and
sliding and made some gestures with his hands.  No, he didn't want to go back
out again.  

The last run group of the day (my 12th of the weekend, having put 450 track
miles on the odometer) there was a sudden loud bang as I came out of turn 10. 
Look Ma!  No power...Clank Clank Crunch...I coasted to the side of the road. 
Flaggers came rushing over with their dreaded chemical fire extinguishers poised. 
I grabbed the Halon extinguisher from behind the passenger seat and put out the
flames myself.  After pushing the car into the pits we discovered the minor
problem that had caused the fire.  It had to do with the bottom half of the
motor.  Apparently it was still at turn 10.

By the end of January, three weeks later, I was dropping in a freshly rebuilt
motor I had obtained just in time for the first event in February, also Car Guys. 
Installation began on Monday and continued non-stop until Wednesday
afternoon.  Harry and Rich stopped by to witness the start up, a fuel leak was
fixed, and the car was ready to drive.  Now all I had to do was put on 1000 miles
by Saturday morning.  I drove everywhere but the task was done.  I had
accumulated 1125 miles when I pulled into Summit Point raceway on Saturday
morning.  

Events in February and March went without a hitch.  In late April I decided to
challenge the 29 degree banking at Charlotte. I had brought an extra pair of tires
just in case.  After the first day the rear tires were shot so they were replaced
with the brand new pair.  The track was intermittently wet and dry as showers
blew across in and out.  Even so, we were able to reach speeds of 145 to 150
mph.  On Sunday afternoon the track was completely dry and the sun was
shining.  I was out in the first afternoon run session of instructors.  Just after
hitting the apex of banking, and heading out to turn 4, the outermost point of the
curve, the right front tire decided to shed it's steel belt.  The noise was terrific. 
The steering wheel shook.  Debris flew in every direction.  I managed to keep
control and coast into the pits.  Corner workers were amazed.  My inner fender
was gone and the fender had a lump on the top of it the size of a half-football. 
I was happy.  I bolted on one of the extra tires and drove home.

In June I was again at Summit Point with the Car Guys.  Again the weekend
went fantastically. I turned times seconds faster than I had the previous February
even though I was running less boost and therefore less power.  The last run of
the day, Sunday, I was just about to catch John Chehaske, who I had been
chasing all season.  He was just ahead of me, approaching the turn 10 braking
zone.  SSSSSSSSPPPPPOP!  Whooops!  The world spun by as I skittered off the
track in a cloud of steam.  The car came to a rest, luckily without hitting
anything.  

When I got out of the car I noticed four other cars, two to each side perfectly
arranged, had followed me off the track.  Apparently the head gasket blew with
such force all the coolant (which is actually slicker than oil) spewed out of the
overflow tank and onto my rear tires.  John Chehaske later said that the
strangest thing he ever saw was my tail lights closing on him in the rear view
mirror as he entered turn 10.  The car was on the road by Wednesday of the
same week.

In late June I decided that my CV-joints were on the way out so I replaced the
left pair.  I got bored so I yanked the transmission and replaced all the synchros. 
While I was at it I threw in two more CV joints.  More Summit Point events. 
For six hot grueling days at Watkins Glen the car held together.

Then came August.  A little off road excursion landed my car in the body shop. 
I missed all track events in September, October and most of November. 
Thousands of dollars and more than three months later I got the car back, just
in time for a Car Guys event.  One small problem.  The shop had neglected to
install one of the front brake pads.  None could be found (this was 9 pm on the
Wednesday before Thanksgiving, the track event was Saturday) so I had to drive
the Honda.  Brake pads were gone by noon each day, even though they had been
replaced each morning.

The car has been back to the shop two more times, and will go back a few more
before the March 2-3 event.   Can't wait!

Steve Timmins

--

Subject: 914 Door Handle Fix
Summary: "door handle fix" isn't what you want but there's another solution

   From: Robert Merithew <merithew@jessica.stanford.edu>
   Date: Sun, 6 Jan 91 11:56:56 PST
   X-Mailer: ELM [version 2.3 PL8]

   I've noticed that both Automobile Atlanta and Zims Autotechnik offer
   inexpesive kits called '914 door handle fix' ( < $15).  Does anyone
   know what these are?

   [ . . . ]
   The tab on the door handle to which this second bolt attaches is
   broken off.  Judging by the design of the handle, this looks like
   it is probably a common occurence.

There are two very popular ways for 914 outside door latches to break.
I've had fairly extensive experience with both :-(

The easier-to-fix problem is not the one you have, instead it happens
when a plastic piece of the latch mechanism breaks.  If you take a
latch off the car you'll see a plastic piece (a ring with two little
arms) around the lock cylinder which moves in and out when you pull
the latch.  This piece then pushes on the metal linkage inside the door.

The "914 door handle fix" is a replacement for this plastic piece.
You get it on (and the old one off) by slightly bending the metal
forks that move the plastic piece in and out.  You can stop by
Automotion in Santa Clara (I see you're at Stanford) if you want to
see or buy one; I've used this fix once (with perfect results).  It
turns out that my other door latch has its plastic piece cracked right
now as well, although it still works fine (but I have an extra "door
handle fix" for when it finally breaks all the way...)

The second possible door latch problem is the one you have: the pot
metal rear tab breaks completely off the latch.  The only "real" way
to fix this is with a new latch (you can save your old key cylinder if
you want to use your old keys).  New latches are fairly expensive, so
I came up with a sort of kludgey fix (which has been working fine for
over a year on both of my current door handles).

The kludge is to replace the the broken-off metal tab with a flat
piece of steel about 1" by 3/8" and 1/16" thick.  You attach this
piece to the latch by wedging it in between the latch spring and the
latch's outer plate.  You attach it to the door by drilling a hole
near the end, and using a bolt and a couple of nuts to act like the
threaded stud on the end of the original tab.  This fix doesn't at all
interfere with the operation of the latch (use a little graphite
lubricant on the latch spring and pivot).

I actually found the perfect piece of metal to use for this in a
hardware store -- I picked up a set of "door hinge brackets" (I think)
for a buck or so.  They already had holes at the ends, and all I had
to do was hacksaw them to the right length.

Well, this fix is inelegant and looks funny close up, but (1) you
don't notice it unless you're looking for it, (2) it works fine, and
(3) it's around $80 or so per latch cheaper than the real fix.

Good luck,

- David Brown       dab@esl.com     (408) 738-2888 x6695

The "door handle fix" is really a replacment plastic slider that is
acuated by the handle to effect a release of the latch mechanism. Not
what you need, which is a complete new handle.

When I replaced my door handles (they broke i.e. couldn't open either
door one day when I had taken my  boss to lunch in the car. Not fun.)
the best price I got was from Automechanika in Denver (Englewood, actually,
but it's a 'burb...). I think I paid about $120 a side for new handles.
And then I took the old handles with the new to a locksmith, and had the
new guys re-keyed to the old key for about $30. Well worth the investment.

And consider replacing both at the same time. It's not too expensive, and
if one failed, the other is just waiting to happen...

Stan

It is a good idea to check the specs of the valve springs. Several
Mailorder places for Porsche parts sell aftermarket springs for 911
engines that do not meet specs. The factory springs are of very high
quality. It is correct that they used the same springs from 65. They
are just adjusted to different valve sizes, cams and rpms by
shimming them to a specified preload by putting spacers underneith
the lower valve spring retainer. 
I do have some of the factory valve specs somewhere at home from an old 
german factory bulletin. I'll try to find it.

Frank

that often breaks, particularly in the cold. 

I broke the stud that is part of the "tab" you are referring to.  I
fixed that by drilling through the tab and installing a bolt that I had
ground most of the head off.  If you have broken off the tab, you may
have no choice but to buy a new handle assembly.

Subject: '73 914 question

I have a '73 914 2.0 which I am preparing to autocross in SCCA Solo II Stock
category.  This will be a limit-of-the-rules car, and I have something I need
to verify as the actual limit here.

In "The 914 & 914/6 Porsche - A Restorer's Guide to Authenticity" by Dr. B.
Johnson, on page 26 under "Door, Rocker Panels", it reads:

"The inner structure [of the door] was substantially modified early in the
1973 model year, due to the change in window channels and lift mechanism.  A
subsequent alteration later in 1973 saw the incorporation of steel side
guards, as dictated by U.S. safety standards.  These weighty additions were
not found on non-U.S. specification cars."

Also, on page 35 under "Engine Compartment and Rear Chassis", it reads:

"In mid-year 1973 at chassis number 473 291 0937 U.S. cars received an
alteration described as a protection rail.  This was a triangular
reinforcement at the base of the roll bar which added rigidity.  Its
appearance conincided with the addition of steel beams in the doors mandated
by U.S. side-impact regulations."

Can anyone verify that there actually were '73 914 2.0's available in the
U.S. without steel bars in the doors?  If this is true, then I can take them
out of my car, which has them.  But I need to verify that this is actually
the case before I go ahead and do it, as this is not an authentic Porsche
publication and will not serve as proof.  Or can anyone recommend a good way
to find out?

And while I'm on the subject, anyone know where I can find an original
factory service manual for this car, and/or some official documentation on
engine specs and the like (for instance, how far can I mill the heads?)

Thanks muchly in advance.

--

University of Rochester, Rochester NY

>It's an '84 944.  I got the gap specs at: .028-.032 and

>the threads.  Then tighten to 18-22 ft.lbs.  Is this cool?

This is cool.  Of course, the cylinder head should also be cool when
you do the deed.  I use a product called Never-Seez on the plug
threads.  You will also want to take this opportunity to check the
condition of the plugs you pull.  Various sources (e.g.  Haynes
manuals) have color pictures of normal and abnormal plug "faces".

From: tynor@prism.gatech.edu (Steve Tynor)
Message-Id: <9101111535.AA12607@prism.gatech.edu>
To: porschephiles@bcm.tmc.edu
Subject: Update: 951 stumbling / 912 soft targa 

Re: my recent query regarding my 951's early morning stumbling under
acceleration: see this month's (Jan) Pano Q&A. A reader describes my problem
almost word for word. The answer: this is very common! It is probably dirty
valves. Fix: blast w/ walnut shells and use Techron regularly to prevent
further buildup on the valves and injectors. I've taken my car in for
inspection and none of the other possibilities (eg. bad cold-start valve, bad
mixture adjustment) panned out. I'm going to run Techron for another couple of
months, and if it doesn't get any better, I'll probably end up feeding the
valves some walnuts.

Re: my negotiations for what I thought was the 'deal of the decade', a '67 912
soft window Targa: After much haggling and mind games, I took the car for a
final inspection by the local 356 guru and much to my dismay, found out that
the car was a rolling heap. While the running gear and interior was in good
shape, the engine block had been welded, the floor pan had been replaced with a
non-porsche pan, and the body was not nearly as straight as I'd thought. It was
so bad, that I was somewhat embarrassed for having even bothered having it
inspected... Oh well. More than anything, I'm glad I didn't make a the mistake
of buying it and having to soak many thousands of $$ into it and "never getting
it right" (the mechanics words). The article on the '73 911S from hell in this
months Pano hit home.  Still in love with that body style - hope I can find a
'67-69 soft-window 911 or 912 that I can actually afford...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=
Porsche: there is no substitute.

>So I want to change my spark plugs...  Still looking
>for some antiseize but the question is what do I do
>for a torque wrench.  I don't want to tighten them too
>far you know.  I don't think I want to buy one unless
>there are some real good reasons for me to have one.
>Does anybody out there who doesn't have one do something
>special as a compromise?
 
I heard an interesting one about this not too long ago (probably got it off of
the net here somewhere).  The plug should be tightened to finger tight, making
sure to mark the direction of the _open_ end of the electrodes.  Then tighten
(and crush the copper washer) with a wrench until the opening of the
electrodes point _towards_ the intake valve(s).  Now, I don't think it matters
much where they point, because there is a compression stroke between the
intake and the power strokes, but I don't know...  Still it always pays to
play it safe, especially if it doesn't cost you anything...
 
Just my opinion...
 
Al Bowers    bowers@elxsi.dfrf.nasa.gov

From sync  Wed Jan 23 21:36:05 1991
Received: by karazm.math.uh.edu id <AA00735@karazm.math.uh.edu>; Wed, 23 Jan 91 21:36:08 CST

From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
To: Rich Core <richc@metaware.com>
To: Bruce Anderson <0002549486@mcimail.com>
Cc: porschephiles <porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu>
Subject: Re: gas w alcohol
Message-Id: <15910124031951/0004056890NB4EM@mcimail.com>

If that respected voice is Bruce Anderson, I would take his word for it!  The
problem with alcohol is that it DOES mix with water, and will take water out of
your tank and into the fuel lines and injectors.  I believe there is some risk
that that water can a) cause corrosion in the lines if it sits there, and b)
either by itself or because of other things mixed in with it, clog injectors.

I am no expert in these matters.  Bruce had a write-up on alcohol in a recent
issue of Pano or Excellence (Bruce: where was it?).  My understanding is that
Robert Bosch AG recommends against use of alcohol either as a regular gas
additive (gasohol) or as a one-shot bolus to get water out of a tank of gas. 
The problem is, if you do have water in your tank, there's not much else you can
do short of disassembling a cleaning the tank, lines, pump, etc.  Anyone know
better?

It seems unlikely that alcohol based fuels could deliver better performance. 
The following chart is excerpted from Bosch's Automotive Handbook, 2ed.

                              specific heating    theoretial air
                              value MJ/kg         requirements kg/kg
Spark-ignition engine fuel   
  regular gasoline              42.7              14.8
  premium gasoline              43.5              14.7

iso-octane                      44.6              15.2

ethanal                         26.8               9
methanol                        19.7               6.4

OK, I'm no chemical engineer, but it looks to me like the alcohols yield about
half as much energy by weight, and require a very different air/fuel mixture. 
So how you would get better performance is unknown to me.

Kevin Gross

From stan  Thu Jan 24 00:16:05 1991
Received: by karazm.math.uh.edu id <AA01656@karazm.math.uh.edu>; Thu, 24 Jan 91 00:16:05 CST
Received: by karazm.math.uh.edu id <AA01616@karazm.math.uh.edu>; Thu, 24 Jan 91 00:12:44 CST
Date: Thu, 24 Jan 91 00:12:44 CST
From: Stan Hanks (bcm) <stan>
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu, richc@metaware.com
Subject: Re:  gas w alcohol

Corky pretty much hit this one straight up. Alcohol is hygroscopic (and
everyone says there's no win in marrying literary types 8{) ) which means
that it 'absorbs'  water. So, you wind up with the alcohol and water and
assorted other goo that the resulting mess disolves from the sides of your
gas tank settling to the bottom of the tank, as water is heavier than gas.
But hey, guess where your fuel pump   pickups are??  Right!!!

With carburetted engines, it's not a big deal: some water goes into the
carb, and gets atomized with the  gas, and you sort of get this "gasp"
from the engine, then you start  getting real gas  again and you go on.
With an injected engine, you squirt water right into the cylinder, shorting
the plug. Next time through, it's still  wet, so you squirt some more gas in
there and it gets wetter. You usually wind up missing on that cylinder
until you stop it for a while and things have  a chance to evaporate.

The other big  gotcha is that with timed  injection systems like the Bosche
L found in the 928 and the 1.7 and 2.0 L 914s, and their big brother the DME 
(LH-E) found inthe Carreras, that you can wind up with gunk sticking in the
moving parts of the injector and holding it open. Same holds   true for
the earlier mechanical injection cars, too. The SC's, some Turbo's and the 924s
all had CIS which is basicly a contiuously open system. The chance of it
plugging is smaller as a result, but the chances of it being more troublesome
if it does plug are higher. 

FYI, you can buy some stuff from LMI at most parts places (well, most places
that sell *Porsche* parts anyway 8{) ) that is a pressure-fed injection 
cleaning kit. Some fittings that plug in where the pressure side of the
fuel pump fits onto the fuel   distributor, some more  fittings off to a
pressurized can of de-gooper, set it up, start the car, and instant  cleaned
injectors. You mechanic will charge you ~$90 to do this. The manifold adaptor
stuff is about $40, the can of de-gooper about $25. 

Stan

 .

[Kevin: Bruce is off the list by his request. He never contributed, but
it was nice having him around anyway]

Kevin also hit a key point: if you're running pure methanol, you get to
re-time your injection. You really do get shitty milage with it, but the
reason people like it is that you waste less heat and only produce C02
and water vapor as emissions. Some win to that, you know.

I ran a big-block Ford methanol engine in a re-bodied Mustang many, many
years ago when I was stalking 'vettes (before I discovered the joys of
actually being able to stop or to corner!). I got about 4.5 MPG, whereas
I was getting about 7 MPG  in the same block with the high-octane
gas setup. [kids: don't do this in your Porsche. The conversion is *NOT* 
fun]

Stan

The D-Jetronic was the first pulsed EFI system used in Porsches.  It sensed
manifold pressure to infer engine load, ie, how much air is being drawn through
the throttle.  L-Jetronic uses a vane mechanism, same idea as continues to be
used to date in the 944-series cars.  The D's never had a lambda sensor; some
L's did.

The D-Jetronic injector pulses are controlled by trigger contacts in the
distributor.  And they're opened two at a time.

I can't tell you much about CA-ification.

Kevin Gross

In article <142278@pyramid.pyramid.com> you write:
>
>I am planning to buy a used Porsche 911. From looking at the paper, it appears
>I can afford something in the range of 1980-1985. I am looking for
>comments/suggestions/recomendations/flames. Please help.

>Thanks in Advance,

>John Muth

I don't know where you are located, but I have two strong words of
advise:

1) If you can afford it pick up a Carrera (84 +) rather than an SC.  The
SC has a 3.0 ltr engine, with rabbit like (air box and fuel distributor)
fuel injection system which.  Performance upgrades for this system
usually involve upgrade (?!?) to Webers.  The fuel system is more or
ess mechanical (not to be confused with mechanical fuel injection) of
the Bosch Jetronic type.

Carreras, on the other hand, have a DME system (Digital Motor
Electronics) which has integrated fuel and ignition systems, ie, they
work in conjunction with each other from muli-dimensional mappings of
various inputs.  This system is quite reliable, and can be modified by
the replacement of the DME chip with an aftermarket product.  The
carrera has more horsepower (partly due to it's 3.2 ltr displacement)
and partly due to the DME system.

Other than that the cars are pretty similar.  SCs can be had for
substantially less (18K comes to mind, vs about 22-24 for a similar
carrera) but it is important to know the distinction as thay look
identical, excepting the script on the rear deck lid.

2) Get in touch with your local porsche club and with the porschephile
mailing list.  I will CC them so you can get the mail address if
interested.  Let me know if you need PCA contacts.  I am also CCing them
so list members can correct any mistakes I may have made in the above.
I am a water pumper and just know the 911 stuff by osmosis.

Good luck,
	Steve

From: "Kevin C. Gross" <0004056890@mcimail.com>
To: Doug Fields <fields-doug@cs.yale.edu>

The suspension squeaking may come from the sway bar bushings, especially if you
have Weltmeister bars or any harder bushings.  The solution is to take them
apart and re-goop the bushing surface where it meets the sway bar with a
heavy-duty, non-bleeding lubricant such as wheel bearing grease or anti-seize. 
This is periodic maintenance, ie, plan to do it at least once a year or more,
depending on your sensibilities.

Other cold-weather squeaks may come from plastic-plastic interfaces in the car's
interior.  Popular candidates include the spot where the A-pillars meet the
dash, and the sunroof and wind-deflector interface.  A spray silicon lube is
recommended.

Porsche will tell you that squeaking/squealing brakes are a fact of life, and
they're pretty much right.  The biggest cause of brake squeal has been
asbestos-free pads.  Mercedes owners have suffered this for years (and Mercedes
service rep's too.  They get to deal with the stereotypical customers who yell
and scream at them about it, I have been told).  You can sort of solve the
problem by sticking with the asbestos compound pads while they continue to be
available.  Last time I checked, Porsche continued to carry both the asbestos
and non-asbestos pads, part numbers .01 and .02 respectively.

If you have a Turbo S, make sure you have the anti-squeal shims in, between each
caliper and the pad.  You may be able to order them separately or as part of a
brake hardware kit if they are missing.  Otherwise, try smearing a little
anti-seize on the back of the pad and on the sides where they meet the caliper. 

The brake squeal can also come from pads which have not been properly bedded. 
It can be cleared up pretty simply.  Find yourself a lonely road, and do a
little moderate braking to warm yourself and the car up.  Work up to a series of
hard brakings, like 60 mph down to a stop, braking as hard as you comfortably
can.  This has helped a couple of friends eliminate squealing.

I don't know why your car would lack performance in cold weather.  Us
normally-aspirated folks thrive on the colder, denser air!  There are a number
of things which could be the cause, including the air temperature sensor, engine
temperature sensor, vacuum leaks, and so on.

Kevin Gross

One comment on the brake squealing, that blue stuff in an aerosol (sp?)
can, can work wonders!

I think it's made by permatex and called something to the effect of 
Disk Brake Pad (treatment?).

It is an adhesive that is applied to the BACK of the pads, and, once it 
dries, acts as a vibration damper.

I have not yet used it on my SC, but it completely eliminated an VERY 
strange clunking noise on my Subaru.

Subject: Smog Problems

The Potato-Salad-Eatin' Fool writes:

>Hey now.  I have a few questions about smog laws in California.  I own a
'75 914 2.0.  In the book that lists smog requirements it says that this
car needs a catalytic converter if it's a CA car, but doesn't if it's a
federal car.  Is this correct?  My mechanic told that it needs one re-
gardless of being federal or CA imported.  Secondly, how can I determine
whether or not it's a federal or CA car?  I tried checking the VIN at
the DMV, but no one there knows anything.  Everyone seems to not know
enough about this to make a decision on it.  Of course the only time some
one will know enough is two years from now when *I* have to pay for
smog upgrades.

This is one of the "secrets" revealed in "Secrets of the Inner Circle",
which is highly recommended reading (just for entertainment value) even
if you don't own a 356.  Pellow explains how to determine from your
California registration whether or not you have a car which was originally
registered in Calif, and therefore subject to California smog laws.

I don't have a copy (I borrowed JZ's), but there are two things to look
at: the Yr. Sold can be ** or a year.  If it has the asterisks, it was
brought into California from out of state.  The other thing to look at
is the license number.  These can be correlated to the year the car
was registered.  

You can call Pellow and ask him about it (HCP Research, Santa Clara).

-Dave

1) Excellent, but as I have always said, I believe it is only so

anything smelling of breakdown, which accounts for their high service
costs.

start traffic (I presume you're talking about the 2.5l version, others
are even better, even the S).  You may not have power steering, which
I do not think is necessary.  Overall performance is excellent, but
you need the S2 or Turbo for state-of-the-art.  In Australia early
944s (82 to 84, I think) were without emission controls, so are real
sceamers, like mine!  It took an upgrade to the Turbo/S to alleviate
the emission control problem.

As I said above, running costs seem quite low.  Apart from vandalism
I haven't required extraneous service/repairs.

4) I understand the 944 is quite cheap in the U.S.  It seems like a
good way to get in to a Porsche without the cost or fear of the
911.  There is always the 928...

From stan  Tue Jan 29 01:48:32 1991
Received: by karazm.math.uh.edu id <AA01962@karazm.math.uh.edu>; Tue, 29 Jan 91 01:48:32 CST
Received: by karazm.math.uh.edu id <AA01951@karazm.math.uh.edu>; Tue, 29 Jan 91 01:48:12 CST
Date: Tue, 29 Jan 91 01:48:12 CST
From: Stan Hanks (bcm) <stan>
To: merithew@jessica.stanford.edu, porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
Subject: Re:  914 Brake Warning Light

The 914 brake warning light is slaved to the parking brake warning
circuit in pre-'75 cars. That could be your problem. 

The standard test for whether it's the switch or not is to have the
car sitting still, and pump the brake pedal until it's "full", just
like  you're going to bleed the brakes. Turn the ignition on, and
open a bleed nipple on one front caliper (well, have an assistant do it).
Repeat for one rear caliper. If the warning light goes  on, everything
is OK. If it doesn't, bad switch or bad wiring. If that works, but it
still comes on randomly, bad switch or wiring. I'd be inclined to check
the grounds first....

Good luck. 

Stan

P.S. Don't hold me to any of this -- I haven't even *seen* my 914 for 
a few months, much less worked on it....

From sync  Tue Jan 29 09:18:31 1991

	id AA27447; Tue, 29 Jan 91 10:14:37 EST
Message-Id: <9101291514.AA27447@sirius.cs.rpi.edu>
To: merithew@jessica.stanford.edu
Cc: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu
In-Reply-To: Robert Merithew's message of Sat, 26 Jan 91 15:02:01 PDT <9101262302.AA18826@jessica.stanford.edu>
Subject: 914 Brake Warning Light


If the switch is broken, the light will either stay on or not go on.
If it goes on under braking, chances are there is something wrong.

When you pump the pedal, does the light go off?  When you hold the
pedal down, does it stop, or does it slowly move down?

Usually, this light is reporting a problem in the mc.  There are two
basic kinds of leaks in the mc.  You are using the dual circuit mc,
right?  I seem to recall that the 19mm only comes in the dual circuit
version, but I may be wrong on that.  If it is, the first circuit may
be leaking into the second, in which case there would be no visible
leak, just very low pressure in the front brakes.  If the second is
leaking, it may be running along the piston into the passenger
compartment, and you'd have to look at the floor pan under the pedal
housing for the fluid.

The simplest cause, however, would be that the brakes weren't properly
bled.  I'd bleed them again, and then one more time.  If it still goes
on, then take out the mc, dissassmble it, and inspect the pieces.

Does it go on *every time* you hit the brakes?  I know a guy who
intentionlly wired his rear braking lights to his brake failure
circuit.

=====

Hi,
	I was hoping I might bend your collective ears.  I have run into a
problem that has been difficult (for me) to diagnose: the problem is
intermittent.  If "it" would just fail completely I think I'd be able to
determine what "it" is.
	I'd like to try to work this out for myself if I can. I have the
shop manuals, but I've only owned a Porsche for about a year.  I thought
I'd ask for opinions/help, I feel like I'm still learning a lot.

Car:	'72 911T (targa) w/Mechanical Injection.  Elec. ignition. 96K miles.

	~10K since a valve job/top half rebuild.
	The injection pump was rebuilt about 7K ago, and the throttle
	correlations were set once again about 2K ago by a professional.
	It felt pretty strong after all that.

Problem:
	Some time after the engine has been running for a while (after
	"warm up," but the oil temp can be below "normal") the car starts to
	miss - not very much though first gear, but in second gear and beyond
	it is worthless.  It starts off being an irritation, get worse, and
	over the course of about five minutes the car it will begin stall
	out completely on acceleration.  Up until the worst of the "episode"
	if I simply stop trying to accelerate, and let it drop back down
	to idle, it idles fine.  At the worst part of the episode, it won't
	even idle correctly I have to apply the hand throttle to keep it
	going, and even then it coughs, sputters and backfires.  
	If I stop the car for 2-5 minutes, it becomes usable again for
	about 10 min.  If I just pull by the side of the road for 2-5 min.
	with the engine running there is no difference in the performance
	when I drive off again.  This problem started seemingly "overnight"
	during a cold snap down to about freezing overnight (cold for this
	locale).
	The car is fine from an overnight cold start plus the first 
	20-30 minutes (sometimes more).
	

Data:
	If I'm driving along during the worst part of the episode, the engine
	will often die off completely: stepping on the accelerator has 
	absolutely *no* effect - it's as though the engine is either
	getting no spark or no gas.  This is notably different than the
	problems I've experienced with the injection pump.  In the past
	with a misadjusted pump, I could always "power" my way out of the
	rough spot.  If I try to do that under these circumstances, the
	engine will not respond.

	I tried tweaking both mixture screws on the injection pump. I can
	sense the change that adjusting each screw has, one changes the idle
	mixture, the other adjusts the overall mixture at higher RPMs and
	during normal operation I feel the difference in acceleration
	(boy do I).  But in the final analysis, the car will still
	completely stall out during an episode.  I put the adjustments
	back to the original settings.

	I also cleaned and checked the injection pump thermostat and air
	hoses. This had no effect.

	After these checks, I turned away from the injection pump as the
	source of the problem.

	--

	The car is tuned up, and the valve clearances are correct. Plugs
	look just right if you compare them with those pictures in the
	Haynes manual, a little lean if anything.  Before, they were a
	touch carbon fouled (:-).

	Pinching off the cold start line makes no difference.

	I've checked the current drain and the voltage drop on the fuel
	pump and on the electronic ignition box: they are both within the
	specified values in the shop manual.  The coil resistance also
	checks out OK.  

	When cold, the fuel pump puts out the correct volume of fuel
	(1L/30 sec). I don't have a tool that will allow me to check
	the pressure of	the fuel system.  The tool to measure the pressure
	would be about $75, my vacuum/pressure gauge doesn't read high
	enough to measure the pressure correctly.
 
	It doesn't seem to be a catastrophic failure of the low voltage
	side of the electronic ignition because I continue to get a
	reading on the tach during these episodes (the tach is electronic,
	so if the low voltage circuit fails, the tach stops working
	altogether - I tested this).

	So I think I've narrowed it down to a temperature/use dependent
	fault of the fuel pump, high voltage side of the electronic
	ignition, or the coil.

	My first guess would be the fuel pump (It makes a fair amount of
	noise but I don't know if it would be considered excessive or not).

	I think what I should do next is break down and buy the fuel
	pressure gauge to check the system when during an episode, but
	before I do that...
	
	My questions are:

	1) Does this analysis sound reasonable?

	2) Keeping in mind its something of a Heisenbug (short time between
	failing and not failing),  and I am a home mechanic without access
	to fancy devices, what else can	I do to narrow it down further?

	If anyone has had experience with a failure of this kind I'd be
	happy to hear more about what you did to troubleshoot.

	Thanks for your time.

	- Scott Presnell (srp@cgl.ucsf.edu)

As an '88 944 Turbo S owner...

>A question for all the 944 owners:
>
>A friend at work is thinking about buying a 944, as I know only about 911's,
>I need some help on this.
>
>What does everyone think of:
>
>1)      Reliability

After having one for all of (13500-7500 =) 6000 miles, I have found that the car
is rather hardy in that respect. I unfortunately bumped someone in their trailer
hitch (a bane to all 944 with the integrated bumper owners) which although the
only permanent damage was a small scratch on the front bumper, somehow jarred
loose the accelerator linkage... Causing the engine to rev at a min of 4k... It
also knocked the alarm control box into the airbag control, disabling that. Oh
well... That was the fault of the alarm installer (the collision was at < 3mph).
Other than that, I have had no problems in terms of engine, body, or otherwise...
Just the usual Porsche complaint about the damn clear stickers they put in front
of the wheels...

>2)      Drivability

Definately the most fun to drive, IMHO. I have never liked 911s due to their (please
no flames) ungainly and ugly shape, and the 944 feels alot (IMHO, again!) sturdier
on the road. In terms of stop and go, just as any standard driver will tell you,
it is a pain. But once you get onto an open highway (or even a croweded, moving
one) you just hit the cruise to 65 mph and let the car go... Of course, if you want
to have some fun, don't do it while I'm around... Play it safe.

>3)      Cost

High. The repair for that broken linkage I told you about was $170. Oil
changes (done
by Authorized Porsche Dealers) run about $100. The 15000k
checkup/tuneup is $750. Gas
is currently $1.45/93 octane/self/gallon, and the car (the Turbo) gets
VERY GOOD milage
(about 25 in my experience). You pay pretty much, but I have absolute
faith in my dealer
and like the guys over there (I hope it's recripocated :) (Pray
Porsche, Greenwich, CT)
so it's worth it.

>4)      Everything else

Best car Porsche makes for under $80k, unless you are of the
Convertible type (again,
no flames, but I hate 'em). A 928 feels a bit more solid near the top end, but the 
thrill of driving is not the speed, but the acceleration, either it
being 0-80 or just
having the car stick going around a "15 mph" curve at 40. My favorite of their cars.

Doug

Subject: 944 fun...


Answers from a starving student...

As long as the car is paid for, it can be done...

Maintenance is the key...  I've heard it said that the 944 will start
and stay running the first time you turn the ignition EVERY TIME.
No stalling out, no sitting around in the cold weather turning
and turning the starter.  It's true.  Aside from when I had
a battery die on me and a starter cable get loose, I've never
had to turn the key more than once and I was off!  Most car
makers don't understand that.  


As far as price goes.  Yes, you're paying 300% of the price of
an American part (If you go through traditional repair prices.).
And yes, you're paying for more hours because Porsche decided
that the engine must be taken OUT of the car to change the
cam belt, the tires, the spark plugs, and the oil.  :-)  Fortunately,
there's a workaround for removing racoons so you don't have to
remove the engine for that.  And fuses are not too time consuming
to change.  ACTUALLY, it's not that bad!!!  It's the fact that
mechanics won't let YOU buy the parts that really hurts because
there are many cheaper outlets.  So, it's a catch because there's
a great and impossible compromise of paying rock bottom for
parts but not having to put them in the car.  And yes, insurance
can be higher depending on where you live...  But look what you
get for a little larger yearly "service charge".  Sortof like
a "gold card".  Everybody goes "oooh" and "aaaah" as you flash
the CARd and you personally can get so much more out of it than
just the standard CARd and for only a small extra fee.

Which brings me to the next point.  Ooohs and aaaaaahs are deserving
because inside and out, the 944 is one of the only REAL cars in the
world.  I haven't had the privalege of driving the amazing 911 yet
and I can't wait but after test driving all other kinds, I drove the
944 and instantly realized the meaning of automobile.  Basically:

You want to stop:
	It stops.

You want to turn:
	It turns.

You want to go real fast:
	It goes real fast.

Oh, and you want to start:
	It starts...

	NOTE: I think there might be a comparitively high level of
	cockpit noise but I haven't compared with other REAL cars.

There are VERY few cars in the world that can make this claim.  REALLY.
Most manufactures make similar hand wavings but when it really boils
down to it, in most cars, you don't FEEL it.  You hit the brakes and
the car lurches forward, and then throws you back in your seat.  You
call that stopping?  You shimmy the steering wheel 15 degrees in both
directions and the car still moves straight.  You call that steering??
You step on the gas...  Well, you can get plenty of cars that can do
this but without the above two, I personally think it's rather useless!
And we've already discussed reliability...

The only PROBLEM with Porsches (that have been kept in good shape) is
that you can't really ever enjoy another car again (except rental cars).
There will eternally be a Porsche in your driveway, or your future.

It doesn't really matter which car you start with, if it's a Porsche,
the difference will be evident immediately.  But to differentiate between
the 944 and the 911 a little.  The neutral steering of the 944 means
that people afraid of going off cliff backwards don't have to worry.
The front engine and rear wheel drive make it a real charmer.  I mean,
I've got Yokohamas A008s on the car and I've never had a problem and
I'm *trying* to make the darn things lose their grip; heel toeing and
all.  My friend in an 84 RX7 SE, in the rain, has mucho problems with
the same tires.  The difference is the 944.

Soren, tell your friend to enjoy.

James B. Saxon

John -

I both hate and love to contradict you!  In the Boston area, I have been
extremely pleased with work done by Clair on my car.  Clair is a large
dealership in Dedham.  Not only have they done what they said they would, a
mechanin also picked up on a problem with my car's suspension and nipped it in
the bud, before real damage occured.  (The problem was caused by the as-sold
Holbert Cool Brake kit.  I will skip the details...)  Tom Shanahan, Dottie, and
all the mechanics have been very good to me.

Clair has also been very supportive of our PCA Region (which, ahem, you should
join).  Mark Burns and Mac MacKenzie in parts have made a bunch of us into
regular customers for Porsche, VW, and Audi parts.  And if you go into the
Porsche/Audi/Mercedes service area, you'll see local PCA'ers Steve Ross and
Enrique Pinzon behind the Mercedes counter.

Stepping back a moment, I would say there are a number of fine dealerships with
fine service departments in the area.  And, to be honest, there are a number of
not-so-fine ones.  It isn't obvious who they are, good and bad.  No relationship
I can tell between size or longevity, and quality.  There are also some
excellent and some less-than-satisfactory independants.

On the subject of parts, raised by James Saxon, I have this to add.  PCNA
figured out a couple of years ago that they weren't selling too many oil filters
to us types.  So they got real competitive with the aftermarket, and I mean REAL
competitive.  But only where they were getting beat up.  Today, you can buy oil
filters from a dealership for significantly less than Imparts will charge you. 
Ditto for any number of consumables.  But you will still pay a fortune for
little trim pieces, until there's an aftermarket.

Hey, that's what free-market capitalism is all about, right?  PCNA can still
make plenty of dough an be competitive, which is why they've started Porsche
Vintage in the US (and a similar unit worldwide), and why the Porsche family
partly owns Stoddard.

Kevin Gross

I hope that everyone has learned, from this extended debate, that the
quality of auto repair has absolutely no relation to whether the shop
is a dealer or not.  The quality depends ENTIRELY on the mechanics, or
maybe just one, and the service depends on the owner and the service
manager.  For each locality, you must search for a place that has a
good combination of quality and service.  In some places this may be a
dealer, which is unfortunate because dealer's always charge more, and
in some places it may be an independant.

Subject: 911 valve springs and thanks for 944 info!
To: porschephiles@karazm.math.uh.edu


Hi everyone,

After reading todays mail, I thought I ought to send a little something.

This is primarliy to John (pz) regarding valve springs and his comment 
about the factory springs costing 5 or 10 times more than aftermarket.

On the SC's, the 1978 model used springs from a new supplier and had 
a very high failure rate, the 1979's are an unknown, the old supplier
was back, but Porsche decided to use up the stock of bad springs.
The 1980's all had good springs.

What I'm getting at here, is that I don't think I would put anything but
factory springs in a 911 (certainly not in mine!).

This doesn't mean that you can only get factory springs from the dealer, 
but I would be very careful about springs from anyone else.  Mind you, I
do not normally buy from the dealer.

One more point, as I recall, only the inner spring fails.  The symptom is 
that the engine will not rev above 5500 rpm.  The spring may be replace 
w/o engine removal by using a leak-down tester.


Also, I would like to thank everyone that has responded to my 
request for 944 info.


--Soren            laforce@krypton.arc.nasa.gov

I wrote:
>
>I recently replaced the stock master cylinder on my 914 2.0 with
>a 19mm one.  All four wheels seem to brake fine.
>
>The problem is that as soon as I got everything back together, filled
>with fluid, and bled, the brake warning light comes on every time I
>brake.
       oops...   not true.  the light was on all of the time...

>  I checked connections, and measured resistances, and determined
>that the switch on the master cylinder is in fact trying to tell me
>that my brakes are failing.  I've not noticed any loss of fluid or
>brake effectiveness, but I've stopped driving it anyway.
>

The problem was that the new brake failure switch has a feature that
causes the switch to stay on after it gets triggered once.  If you remove
the little rubber cover near the electrical contact and push in the little
plastic protruding rod, the switch is reset.  Unfortunately, I didn't
discover this until I had already swapped in the switch from the old
master cylinder.  I imagine the switch got triggered when I was bleeding
the brakes...

The m/c was a new 19mm from ATE - I don't know if older 19mm cylinders
have this feature...

By the way, one apparently *can* remove the switch without loss of fluid
or introduction of air into the system...

Here's to little successes!

-Robert Merithew
 Dept. of Physics
 Stanford University

John -

Indeed, rebutal is what makes America such a damn fine country!

That any Porsche dealer doesn't have a part in stock is not a problem.  PCNA
ships stuff overnight, and almost anything not in stock does in fact show up the
next day.  You can blame in part the Tax Reform of '86 for this, which (if
memory serves) added an inventory tax.

I cannot really blame a dealer's parts dept for not knowing that all 911
valvesprings are the same.  Who cares, as long as they look up the right part
number on their fiche and order it for you?  They have to deal with a bunch of
different marques, across all years.  Pretty overwhelming.
